Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Defining Divine Will and Refuting the Denial of a Real Attribute
Nasafi defines irada as a real attribute contrary to compulsion and refutes al-Najjar's reductionist view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine will (irada) is a real attribute that actively entails choice, not a mere negation of compulsion.

Core Idea
Divine will is a real meaning, not just the absence of compulsion.

Al-Nasafi begins by presenting the classical definition of irada as a meaning that necessitates choice and contradicts aversion and compulsion. He then refutes al-Najjar's reductionist view that 'will' is merely a negation of certain states, using a logical argument based on the status of accidents. This lesson clarifies that irada is a positive attribute that makes the agent a free chooser.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the two parts: definition and refutation.
2
Identify the definition and its implications
Find how the passage defines irada as a 'meaning' (ma'na) and what that entails (intention and choice). Understand that it is a real attribute, not a mere negation.
3
Understand al-Najjar's position
Note al-Najjar's claim: irada is just the absence of compulsion, forgetfulness, and being overcome. The passage refutes this.
This is a reductionist view.
4
Follow the refutation argument
The argument uses the case of accidents: if irada were mere negation, accidents would be 'willing' because they also lack those negations. Since they are not, irada must be a positive attribute.
5
Clarify al-Maturidi's statement
Understand that al-Maturidi's words are not supporting al-Najjar but showing that negation of opposites requires a real will.
This prevents misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حدها فقد قيل : إنها معنى ينافي الكراهية والاضطرار، ويوجب لمن هي له القصد والاختيار. فكانت فائدتها على هذا التحديد كون الموصوف بها مختارا فيما فعله غير مضطر إليه لوجود ما ينافي الكراهية والاضطرار، لا لانعدام الكره والاضطرار فحسب كما هو المحكى عن النجار، فإنه زعم أن معنى قولنا: إنه مريد أي أنه ليس بمكره ولا ساه ولا مغلوب من غير إثبات وصف له على الحقيقة . وذلك باطل، لأن ذلك يوجب أن تكون الأعراض كلها مريدة لأنها ليست مغلوبة ولا مكرهة ولا ساهية، ولما لم تكن الأعراض مريدة مع انتفاء هذه المعاني عنها، دل أن الإرادة ليست باسم لعدم هذه المعاني، بل هي اسم لمعنى ينافي هذه المعاني، والأعراض يستحيل قيام ذلك لمعنى بها فاستحال كونها مريدة. وما يجري في خلال كلام الشيخ أبي منصور الماتريدي في إبطال قول الكعبي أن الله تعالى ليس بمريد أنك إذا قلت : ليس بمغلوب ولا مكره ولا ساه، فقد أقررت بكونه مريدا . لا يريد بذلك الميل إلى مذهب النجار، بل يريد أن انتفاء هذه المعاني عن الحي لن تكون إلا بإرادة على ما بينا قبل هذا.
As for its definition, it has been said: It is a meaning (ma'na) that is contrary to aversion (al-karahiyya) and compulsion (al-idtirar), and necessitates for the one who has it intention (al-qasd) and choice (al-ikhtiyar). So its benefit according to this definition is that the one described by it is chosen (mukhtar) in what he does, not compelled (mudtarr), because of the existence of what is contrary to aversion and compulsion, not merely due to the absence of aversion and compulsion, as is reported from al-Najjar. For he claimed that the meaning of our saying 'He is willing (murid)' is that He is not compelled, nor forgetful, nor overcome, without affirming a real attribute for Him. And that is false, because that would necessitate that all accidents (a'rad) are willing, because they are not overcome, nor compelled, nor forgetful. And since accidents are not willing despite the absence of these meanings from them, it indicates that 'irada' is not a name for the absence of these meanings, but rather it is a name for a meaning that is contrary to these meanings. And it is impossible for accidents to have such a meaning subsisting in them, so it is impossible for them to be willing. And what occurs in the discourse of Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi in refuting the saying of al-Ka'bi that 'Allah is not willing' — 'When you say: He is not overcome, nor compelled, nor forgetful, then you have affirmed that He is willing' — he does not mean by that an inclination to the school of al-Najjar, but rather he means that the absence of these meanings from a living being is only through a will, as we have explained before this.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of irada (will) and refutes a reductionist view. The author quotes one definition: 'It is a meaning (ma'na) that is contrary to aversion (karahiyya) and compulsion (idtirar), and necessitates intention and choice for the one who has it.' The benefit is that the agent is described as choosing freely, not compelled, because of the presence of this attribute, not merely the absence of its opposites. Al-Nasafi then refutes al-Najjar, who held that saying 'God is willing' only means He is not compelled, not forgetful, and not overcome—without affirming a real attribute. This is false, because if that were the case, then all accidents (a'rad, non-enduring entities) would be 'willing' since they also lack compulsion, forgetfulness, and being overcome. But accidents are not considered willing, so irada must be a real meaning that is contrary to those negated states. Accidents cannot have such a meaning subsist in them, so they cannot be willing. The author also clarifies a statement of Imam al-Maturidi in refuting al-Ka'bi, who denied that God is willing. Al-Maturidi's point in saying 'if you say He is not overcome, compelled, or forgetful, you have affirmed He is willing' is not to endorse al-Najjar's view (that irada is merely negation), but rather to show that the negation of these meanings in a living being can only be through an actual will, as previously explained. Thus, the passage establishes that irada is a real affirmative attribute (sifat thubutiyya) entailing choice, not a mere negation.

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irada)
الكراهية (al-karahiyya)
الاضطرار (al-idtirar)
القصد (al-qasd)
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyar)
المعنى (al-ma'na)
الأعراض (al-a'rad)
النجار (al-Najjar)
الكعبي (al-Ka'bi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine will is merely the absence of compulsion or forgetfulness (like al-Najjar).
Correction: The passage shows that if that were the case, accidents would be willing since they also lack those states. Therefore, will must be a real attribute actively causing choice.

Checkpoint
What argument does al-Nasafi use to refute al-Najjar's definition of irada as mere negation?
A) It leads to anthropomorphism.
B) It would imply that accidents are willing.
C) It contradicts the Quran.
D) It makes God's will temporal.

Key Takeaway
Divine will is a real, positive attribute of Allah that entails choice, not a mere negation of its opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where your own will is completely frustrated, do you genuinely believe that Allah's will is a real, active attribute that overcomes all obstacles? How does that belief shape your immediate emotional response—do you surrender or resist?
?
Al-Najjar reduced Allah's will to a mere 'not being compelled'. In your daily thoughts, do you ever treat Allah's will as merely passive permission rather than active decree? What would change in your prayer life if you truly internalised that His will is a real, creative force?
?
The argument shows that if will were just negation, even accidents would have it. Have you ever considered that your own human will is not just the absence of coercion but a real attribute gifted by Allah? How does that accountability press on you the next time you make an excuse for sin?

Divine Attributes

Irada and Mashiyya: One Divine Attribute
Nasafi defines irada as the attribute that explains orderly choice, and equates it with mashiyya, refuting the Karramiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Sunni kalam, irada and mashiyya are synonymous terms for the divine attribute of will, and you will recognize the Karramiyya deviation from this consensus.

Core Idea
Irada and mashiyya are two names for the same divine attribute in mainstream kalam.

After establishing that the orderly differentiation in creation proves a divine attribute of will (irada), Nasafi clarifies the terminology used by theologians. He explains that irada and mashiyya are synonyms, except for the Karramiyya who wrongly separate them. This lesson examines that definition and the sectarian dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Nasafi's own argument flow through before you interpret.
Notice the logical progression: proof of irada, then definition, then synonymy.
2
Understand the proof of irada
Nasafi argues that orderly differentiation among homogeneous things proves the agent acts with will. Reflect on why no time, state, or quality is inherently more appropriate for an object without a willing agent.
This is a classic kalam argument for the necessity of divine will.
3
Grasp the synonymy and the Karramiyya deviation
Identify that all mutakallimun except the Karramiyya treat irada and mashiyya as one attribute. Note the Karramiyya claim that mashiyya is eternal and irada is temporal, and why Nasafi rejects it.
The Karramiyya view leads to anthropomorphism (temporal accidents in God).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتوالي، وعلى النظام والاتساق، وعلى الهيئات المختلفة والصفات المتباينة على حسب ما تقتضيه الحكمة البالغة، كان ذلك دليلا على اتصاف الفاعل بالإرادة، لما كان وقت لوجوده أولى من وقت، ولا هيئة أولى من غيرها، ولا صفة ولا كمية ولا كيفية أولى مما سواها. ثم إن هذا المعنى الذي يحصل به هذه المعاني سمي إرادة، لما أن الفاعل إذا فعل شيئا في وقت وعلى هيئة وصفة فقد طلب هذا الوجه لهذا المفعول دون ما سواه من الوجوه، أو مال إلى تخصيصه بهذا الوجه دون غيره من الوجوه ثم إن المراد بلفظة الإرادة عند المتكلمين هذا المعنى الذي بينا وعرفناه بأثره وهو ما مر من كون الموصوف بها مختارا في فعله، أو خروج مفعولاته على وجه دون وهي بعينها المشيئة عند المتكلمين. فالإرادة والمشيئة عندهم لفظان ينبئان عن معنى واحد لم يفرق بينهما أحد من المتكلمين إلا الكرامية، فإنهم يزعمون أن المشيئة صفة الله تعالى أزلية وهي صفة واحدة تتناول ما يشاء الله بها من حيث حدث، فأما الإرادة فهي غير المشيئة وهي عندهم حادثة في ذات القديم تعالى الله عن ذلك علوا كبيرا وله إرادات كثيرة على عداد المرادات يحدث كل إرادة منها قبل حدوث ما هو المراد به، ثم يتعقبها حدوث ما هو المراد بها.
And [the existence of] succession, order, coherence, different states and diverse attributes according to what perfect wisdom requires, that is evidence of the agent being described with will (al-irāda), since there is no time more appropriate for its existence than another time, nor is any state more appropriate than another, nor any attribute, quantity, or quality more appropriate than others. Then this meaning by which these meanings are obtained is called will (irāda), because when the agent does something at a time and in a state and attribute, he has sought this aspect for this effect to the exclusion of other aspects, or inclined to specify it with this aspect rather than other aspects. Then the meaning of the term 'irāda' according to the mutakallimūn is this meaning that we have explained and known by its effect, namely that the one described thereby is free in his action, or that his effects come out in one way rather than another, and it is identical to mashī'a according to the mutakallimūn. So irāda and mashī'a with them are two terms that indicate one meaning; none of the mutakallimūn differentiated between them except the Karāmiyya. Indeed, they claim that mashī'a is an eternal attribute of God Most High, one attribute that encompasses what God wills by it in terms of what occurs, while irāda is other than mashī'a and is according to them an accident in the essence of the Eternal—exalted is God above that, greatly—and He has many irādāt corresponding to the objects of will, each irāda occurring before the occurrence of that which is intended by it, then the occurrence of that which is intended by it follows it.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of will (al-irāda) and clarifies its relationship to the term mashī'a. Nasafi begins by concluding a proof: the orderly succession and differentiation among homogeneous objects, with no inherent preference for any particular time, state, or quality, demonstrates that the agent acts with will. He then explains that the meaning that brings about these specific characteristics is called 'irāda'. The definition is functional: an agent who performs an act at a specific time, in a specific state, with a specific attribute, has chosen that particular aspect for the effect, or inclined toward it. According to the mutakallimūn, the term 'irāda' refers to this meaning—that the agent is choosing (mukhtār) in his action, or that his effects emerge in one manner rather than another. Importantly, irāda and mashī'a are synonymous for all mutakallimūn except the Karāmiyya. The Karāmiyya distinguish between them: mashī'a is an eternal attribute by which God wills all things from eternity, while irāda is a temporal accident (ḥādith) occurring in God's essence before the creation of each thing He wills. Nasafi refutes this view by indicating it is an innovation, stating that all other mutakallimūn (i.e., Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila) consider them one.

Key Terms
إرادة
مشيئة
كرامية
متكلمون
حادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Irada and mashiyya are two different divine attributes in Sunni theology.
Correction: According to the vast majority of mutakallimun (including Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila), irada and mashiyya are synonymous terms for the same eternal attribute of divine will. The Karramiyya alone separated them, but their view is rejected.

Checkpoint
According to the majority of mutakallimun (theologians), what is the relationship between irada (will) and mashiyya (will)?
A) Irada is eternal and mashiyya is temporal.
B) They are two different attributes that work together.
C) They are synonymous terms for the same meaning.
D) Irada refers to command, mashiyya refers to desire.

Key Takeaway
Irada and mashiyya are one and the same divine attribute according to the vast majority of Muslim theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is single and eternal, encompassing everything that occurs, how does that confront your moments of questioning why certain events happen in your life? Will you trust His single will even when you don't understand?
?
The Karāmiyya claimed that God has many temporal wills for each created thing. Do you ever treat God's will as if it changes or adapts to your prayers, rather than submitting to His eternal decree? Today, will you repent of that subtle arrogance?
?
Nasafi uses the order and diversity of creation to prove God's will. Look at one messy area of your own life—your relationships, your failures. Can you see that even chaos serves a wise, choosing will? Will you surrender your need to control that area today?

Disagreement on Describing God with Will
Nasafi reports the dispute between majority and Mu'tazila on God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute between the Sunni majority and the Mu'tazila regarding the attribute of will (irāda) and how the Mu'tazila reinterpret it metaphorically.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila deny a real will in God but reinterpret it as action or command.

Nasafi presents a key theological disagreement: do we describe God with a real will, or only metaphorically? This passage lays out both positions and the Mu'tazilite proof based on equating will with appetite. Understanding this debate clarifies the Sunni commitment to real divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two groups
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Identify the two opposing views: the majority (جمهور الأمة) and the Mu'tazila (al-Naẓẓām and the Baghdadis).
Notice that the majority includes Ash'arites, Maturidis, and most Sunnis.
2
Examine the Mu'tazila interpretation of 'willed'
See how the Mu'tazila explain 'God willed X' in two cases: when X is God's own action, it means He did it without heedlessness or coercion; when X is another's action, it means He commanded it.
This shows they do not completely negate the term but reinterpret it.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila proof
Their proof equates will (irāda) with appetite (shahwa). Since God cannot have appetite, He cannot have will in the real sense. Reflect on why this argument is problematic from a Sunni perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا علمت الإرادة بحدها وحقيقتها فنقول : اختلف الناس في جواز وصف الله تعالى بالإرادة، فقال جمهور الأمة : إن الله تعالى موصوف بالإرادة على الحقيقة. قول النظام مع بعض المعتزلة] وقال النظام والبغداديون من المعتزلة كالكعبي وأستاذه أبي الحسين ومن تابعهم : إن الله تعالى لا يوصف بالإرادة على الحقيقة بل يوصف بها بطريق المجاز. فإذا قيل : أراد الله تعالى كذا ، فإن كان ذلك فعله فمعناه أنه فعل وهو غير ساه ولا مكره عليه ولا مضطر ، وإن كان ذلك غير فعل الله تعالى فمعناه أنه أمر به واستدلوا على ذلك بقولهم : إن الإرادة هي الشهوة ، فلو كان الله تعالى مريدا لكان مشتهيا ، وحيث لم يكن مشتهيا دل أنه ليس بمريد.
Then, when you know the definition and reality of will, we say: People differed on the permissibility of describing God Most High with will. The majority of the community said: God Most High is described with will in reality. The view of al-Naẓẓām with some of the Mu'tazila: And al-Naẓẓām and the Baghdadis from the Mu'tazila, like al-Ka'bī and his teacher Abū al-Ḥusayn and those who followed them, said: God Most High is not described with will in reality, but rather is described with it metaphorically. So if it is said: 'God Most High willed such-and-such,' if that is His action, its meaning is that He did it while not being heedless, nor coerced, nor compelled; and if that is not an action of God Most High, its meaning is that He commanded it. And they argued for that by saying: Will is appetite; so if God Most High were willing, He would have appetite, and since He does not have appetite, it indicates that He is not willing.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage presents a disagreement regarding the description of God with will (al-irāda). Nasafi first states the position of the majority (jumhūr al-umma), which includes the Ash'arites, Maturidis, and most Sunnis: God is described with will in reality (ʿalā al-ḥaqīqa), meaning it is a real attribute. He then reports the view of the Mu'tazila, specifically al-Naẓẓām and the Baghdad school (al-Baghdādiyūn) such as al-Ka'bī and Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī. They deny that God has a real will and interpret it metaphorically (bi-ṭarīq al-majāz). Their hermeneutics: when 'willed' (arāda) is attributed to God regarding His own action, its meaning is that He did it without being forgetful, coerced, or compelled. When it refers to another's action, it means He commanded it. Their proof: they equate will with appetite (shahwa), arguing that if God were willing, He would have appetite (which is impossible). Nasafi does not refute them here but presents the view as part of the doctrinal exposition.

Key Terms
جمهور الأمة
النظام
بغداديون
كعبي
أبو الحسين
مجاز
شهوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila completely deny any meaning to God's will; they negate it entirely.
Correction: They do not negate it entirely but reinterpret it metaphorically as action or command.

Checkpoint
According to the Baghdad Mu'tazila, what does 'God willed X' mean when X is not God's own action?
A) God desired X.
B) God created X.
C) God commanded X.
D) God permitted X.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila reinterpret God's will as action or command to avoid attributing appetite to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself reinterpreting a clear command of God (like 'He wills good for you') into mere metaphor because the idea of a detailed divine will feels uncomfortable or too intrusive into your personal choices? If so, in what way are you following the Mu'tazila's method?
?
When you face a trial, do you truly believe that God willed it specifically for you—not just permitted it—or do you subtly reduce His will to a general permission? How would your response to hardship change if you fully embraced that God's will is real and particular?
?
The Mu'tazila reduced God's will to action and command to avoid attributing appetite to Him. Are you guilty of reducing God's attributes to fit your own limited understanding of what is 'appropriate' for Him, rather than accepting what He revealed about Himself? What would it cost you to surrender your intellectual reservations and accept His attributes as He describes them?

Nasafi's Proof for God's Will
Nasafi provides a logical proof for God's will from specification of homogeneous acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's logical proof that God possesses will, based on the specification of homogeneous acts and the analogy with human agency.

Core Idea
Homogeneous effects differ by will, not essence.

Nasafi presents a compelling argument for the divine attribute of will (irāda). He starts from the observable fact that God's creations are similar in kind yet distinct in their specific features. This differentiation cannot arise from their essences alone, so it must be due to a will that chooses one possibility over another. The argument also draws a parallel to human acts of will, like building or writing, and shows that without will, the agent would be compelled and thus incapable — which cannot be said of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic once to get the flow, then read the translation to understand the meaning. Pay attention to key terms like 'دليل', 'إرادة', and 'مفعولات متجانسة'.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Understand the core observation
The argument begins by noting that God's effects are homogeneous yet differ in attributes, places, and times. Ask: why can't this be from the essences? The essences are equal in possibility.
Homogeneous means similar in kind, e.g., all humans are human.
3
Follow the inference to will
Since the differences are not necessitated by the essences, they must come from a will that specifies them. This is the key step: 'فعلم أن ذلك كان لإرادة الفاعل'.
Will is the specifying principle.
4
Consider the analogy and the alternative
Nasafi uses the analogy of human will in witnessed acts and then argues that without will, the agent would be compelled (muḍṭarr) and thus incapable, which is impossible for God.
This strengthens the case for will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليلنا على أن الله تعالى مريد وقوع مفعولاته المتجانسة على أوصاف مخصوصة يجوز وقوعها على غيرها، وكذا على هيئات مخصوصة في أمكنة مخصوصة وأزمنة مخصوصة، فيعلم عند تجانسها أن وقوعها على هذا الاختلاف في هذه الوجوه لم يكن من اقتضاء ذواتها، فعلم أن ذلك كان لإرادة الفاعل ذلك لولا هي لما وقعت على تلك الوجوه مع مساواة غيرها في الوجوه إياها في الجواز. وبهذا يستدل على كون الفاعل في الشاهد مريدا لما فعله كالبناء والكتابة وغيرهما، ولأنه لو لم يكن مريدا لكان مضطرا في أفعاله، والمضطر عاجز محل لنفوذ قدرة
Our evidence that God Most High is willing: that His homogeneous effects occur with specific attributes while it is possible for them to occur with others, and similarly with specific forms in specific places and specific times. So when they are homogeneous, it is known that their occurrence with this difference in these aspects was not from the requirement of their essences. Thus it is known that that was due to the will of the agent; otherwise they would not have occurred in those aspects, while others are equal to them in the aspects in possibility. And by this one infers the free agent in the witnessed world as willing what he does, like building, writing, and so on. And because if he were not willing, he would be compelled in his actions, and the compelled one is incapable, a locus for the penetration of power.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalīl) for the divine attribute of will (irāda). Nasafi argues from the observable fact that God's effects (mufaʿʿalāt) are homogeneous in essence yet differ in their specific attributes, forms, places, and times. For example, all human bodies are similar, yet each has a unique form and lifespan. This differentiation cannot arise from the essences themselves (since they are equal in possibility), so it must proceed from a will that specifies them. The proof is analogical (qiyās): in the witnessed world (al-shāhid), when we see a builder or writer produce various outcomes with specific characteristics, we infer they act with will. If there were no will, the agent would be compelled (muḍṭarr), and compulsion implies incapacity ('ajz), which cannot be attributed to God. Thus, God must be willing (murīd).

Key Terms
دليل
إرادة
مفعولات متجانسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the proof relies solely on observed human will, making it a direct inference from human to divine.
Correction: The proof uses analogy (qiyās) from the witnessed world (shāhid) to the unseen (ghāʾib), but it is based on a universal principle: specification of homogeneous possibilities requires a will. The human example is illustrative, not the foundation.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for concluding that God's homogeneous effects are due to will?
A) Their essences require diversity.
B) They occur by necessity.
C) They differ in attributes and times despite being homogeneous, and their essences do not necessitate such difference.
D) They are all created at once.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's proof demonstrates that the differentiation of homogeneous effects points to a will that specifies them, and this inference is strengthened by analogy to human willing and the impossibility of divine compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the vast diversity in creation—different faces, life spans, talents—do you truly believe each detail was willed by God, or do you sometimes attribute differences to chance or nature? Examine your heart: where do you place the determining cause?
?
The proof states that if God were not willing, He would be compelled (muḍṭarr) and thus incapable. In your own life, when you act without consciously willing an action—perhaps out of habit or impulse—do you realize that you are acting as a compelled, even incapable agent? How does this remind you of your need to align your will with God’s will?
?
Consider one area of your life where you have made choices—your career, your relationships, your worship. Do you acknowledge that even these choices are subsumed under God’s will? If you believe God wills all things, how does that change your view of the outcomes you are dissatisfied with? Will you trust His will more than your own?

Divine Attributes

Refuting that God's will is desire
Nasafi refutes the identification of divine will with desire (shahwa).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the equation of divine will with desire (shahwa) is invalid, and how Nasafi distinguishes between God's will applied to His own acts and His commands.

Core Idea
God's will is not a passion—it is either creative action or command.

This lesson tackles a common objection: if 'will' means 'desire', then God cannot have will because desire implies imperfection. Nasafi reframes the discussion by showing that the word 'will' is used metaphorically for God. When we say 'Allah willed something', it either means He created it (if it is His own act) or He commanded it (if it is a human act). This clarifies that divine will is not a passion but a sovereign decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then follow with the English translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement followed by an opponent's argument.
The passage is concise but rich in theological reasoning.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'will'
Look for how Nasafi explains the phrase 'Allah willed X' in two contexts: when X is Allah's own act and when X is not His act. What does it mean in each case?
This is the key to understanding the refutation.
3
Analyze the opponent's argument
The opponent equates will with desire (shahwa). Why does this lead to the conclusion that God cannot be described as willing?
Desire implies need and change, which are impossible for Allah.
4
Understand the refutation
By distinguishing the two meanings, Nasafi shows that the opponent's equation is a category mistake. God's will is not a passion but an attribute of action or command.
This illustrates the principle that theological terms must be understood metaphorically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا قيل : أراد الله تعالى كذا ، فإن كان ذلك فعله فمعناه أنه فعل وهو غير ساه ولا مكره عليه ولا مضطر ، وإن كان ذلك غير فعل الله تعالى فمعناه أنه أمر به واستدلوا على ذلك بقولهم : إن الإرادة هي الشهوة ، فلو كان الله تعالى مريدا لكان مشتهيا ، وحيث لم يكن مشتهيا دل أنه ليس بمريد.
If it is said: 'Allah willed such-and-such', if that is His act, then its meaning is that He did it, and He is not heedless, nor coerced, nor compelled; and if that is not Allah's act, then its meaning is that He commanded it. And they argued for that by their saying: 'Will is desire.' So if Allah were willing, He would have desire; and since He does not have desire, it indicates that He is not willing.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes a theological objection. The objector argues that if 'will' means 'desire', then God cannot be described as having will, because desire implies passion, which is impossible for God. Nasafi counters by clarifying that the word 'will' when applied to God is used metaphorically (bi-tariq al-majaz). When we say 'Allah willed X', if X is His own action, it means He performed it without heedlessness, coercion, or compulsion. If X is not His action (e.g., a human action), it means He commanded it. The objector's equation of will with desire is thus invalid, because God's will is not a passion but a perfection.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الشهوة
المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the term 'will' always implies desire or passion, so God cannot be described as willing.
Correction: Divine will is metaphorical: it either denotes creative action or command, not a passion. Thus, God can be described as willing without implying imperfection.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when the phrase 'Allah willed such-and-such' refers to an action that is not Allah's own act, what does it mean?
A) It means Allah desired that action.
B) It means Allah commanded that action.
C) It means Allah compelled the doer.
D) It means Allah performed that action metaphorically.

Key Takeaway
Divine will is not a passion; it is either creative action or command, and thus the objection that God cannot will because He lacks desire is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's will in your life, do you unconsciously imagine it as a kind of desire, like a human wish? How does this lesson challenge you to purify your concept of God?
?
If God's will is either His action or His command, then every trial you face is either directly from Him (an act) or something He has commanded (and thus permitted). Does this make you more submissive or more resistant? Why?
?
The opponents in this passage argued that because God has no desires, He cannot will. Have you ever doubted God's care because His will didn't align with your desires? What will you do the next time that feeling arises?

Proof that God is willing from variation of homogeneous acts
Nasafi proves God's will by the contingency of attributes in homogeneous acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the variation in homogeneous acts demonstrates that God possesses the attribute of will (irada).

Core Idea
Contingent variation in creation proves a selecting will.

Nasafi presents a rational proof for the divine attribute of will (irada). He observes that God's acts are homogeneous in kind yet vary in specific attributes, forms, places, and times. Since these variations are contingent and not necessitated by the essences of the acts themselves, they must be chosen by a will. This argument also parallels human voluntary actions and refutes the notion of divine compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the key terms: الإرادة, المفعولات المتجانسة, الاقتضاء, المضطر.
The passage is a continuous proof; try to follow the logical flow.
2
Understand the core argument
Identify the premise: homogeneous acts occur with specific variations (attributes, places, times) that could have been otherwise. The conclusion: these variations must be due to a will, not the essences of the acts.
The key phrase is 'لم يكن من اقتضاء ذواتها' (not from necessitation of their essences).
3
Analyze the analogy and reductio
Nasafi draws an analogy to human voluntary actions (building, writing) and then argues that without will, the agent would be compelled, which implies incapacity and origination.
The reductio shows that compulsion is incompatible with divine perfection.
4
Review the translation and commentary
Read the literal translation and the teacher's commentary to ensure you grasp every detail.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'فعلم أن' (so it is known that).
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question and try the activity to solidify the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليلنا على أن الله تعالى مريد وقوع مفعولاته المتجانسة على أوصاف مخصوصة يجوز وقوعها على غيرها، وكذا على هيئات مخصوصة في أمكنة مخصوصة وأزمنة مخصوصة، فيعلم عند تجانسها أن وقوعها على هذا الاختلاف في هذه الوجوه لم يكن من اقتضاء ذواتها، فعلم أن ذلك كان لإرادة الفاعل ذلك لولا هي لما وقعت على تلك الوجوه مع مساواة غيرها في الوجوه إياها في الجواز. وبهذا يستدل على كون الفاعل في الشاهد مريدا لما فعله كالبناء والكتابة وغيرهما، ولأنه لو لم يكن مريدا لكان مضطرا في أفعاله، والمضطر عاجز محل لنفوذ قدرة غيره فيه، إذ ما وجد في المضطر من الفعل محض تعليق غيره لا اكتساب له فيه لا قدرة له عليه، ومن هو بهذه الصفة فهو محدث.
Our proof that Allah is willing: His homogeneous acts occur on specific attributes when it is possible for them to occur on others, and likewise on specific forms in specific places and specific times. So it is known, given their homogeneity, that their occurring with this variation in these aspects was not from the necessitation of their essences. Thus it is known that it was due to the will of the agent; otherwise they would not have occurred in those ways, while others equal them in the aspects in terms of possibility. And by this one proves that the agent in the witnessed world is willing regarding what he does, like building, writing, and others. And because if he were not willing, he would be compelled in his actions, and the compelled person is incapable, a locus for the implementation of another's power, for what occurs from the compelled person is mere attachment of another, no acquisition for him in it, no power over it, and whoever is of this description is originated.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage provides a proof for the attribute of will (irada) in God. Nasafi argues that God's actions (such as creating things) are homogeneous in their essence but differ in specific attributes, forms, places, and times. Since these variations are contingent—each could have been otherwise—they cannot be necessitated by the essences of the things themselves. Therefore, there must be a will that selects one possibility over another. This is analogous to human actions: a builder or writer chooses specific arrangements by his will. If an agent lacked will, his actions would be compelled, which implies incapacity and being subject to another's power. Such impotence is a sign of being originated (hadith), not compatible with God's perfection.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المفعولات المتجانسة
الاقتضاء
المضطر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The variation in creation is due to material causes or chance, not a specific divine will.
Correction: Nasafi argues that since the acts are homogeneous in essence, material causes or chance cannot necessitate specific variations; only a willing agent can select one possibility over another.

Checkpoint
Why does the variation in homogeneous acts indicate the existence of will?
A) Because homogeneity prevents variation.
B) Because variations are due to the essences of things.
C) Because variations are contingent and not necessitated by essence, so a will is needed to specify them.
D) Because the agent is compelled.

Key Takeaway
The contingent variation in homogeneous acts compels the inference of a divine will, without which the agent would be compelled and thus imperfect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the variations in your own life: your place of birth, time, family, abilities. All these could have been otherwise. Does recognizing that they are not from your own essence lead you to see the will of Allah in your life? How does that change your gratitude or submission?
?
The passage argues that if God were not willing, He would be compelled and incapable. Do you sometimes act as if God is indifferent or forced by external factors? How does affirming His will affect your trust in His decree?
?
You are a deliberate agent in your actions (like building or writing). Yet you also acknowledge that your will is secondary to God's will. In what areas of your life do you forget that your will is dependent? Commit to one area where you will consciously attribute your choices to Allah's will.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
8 min
Absurdity of identifying will with the act
Nasafi refutes the claim that will is identical to the act by absurd consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why identifying God's will with the act leads to absurd consequences, and how Nasafi uses the property of 'life' to refute this.

Core Idea
If will were the act, then blackness would be will — an absurdity that destroys the distinction between Creator and creation.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum against the claim that God's will is identical to His act. If that were true, then every created thing would be will, which is clearly false. Then he gives two rigorous arguments based on the concept that will is a property (khasisa) of living beings, just like knowledge and power. This lesson unpacks those arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial hypothetical and its absurd consequences
The passage begins by supposing will equals act. This leads to the absurdity that blackness, whiteness, etc., would all be will. Notice how this violates common sense and theology.
This sets up the reductio.
2
Understand the first argument using analogy with knowledge and power
Nasafi argues: if will were identical to its object, then by analogy knowledge would be identical to the known, and power to the object of power. But that would make contingent things (like blackness) into divine attributes, which is impossible because those things are not properties of a living being.
Focus on the principle of 'properties of the living being' (khasais al-hayy).
3
Grasp the second argument focusing on the property of life
The second argument is more direct: will is a property of the living being, but the objects willed (e.g., blackness) are not. If object willed were identical to will, then those objects would have to be properties of the living being, which they are not. Therefore, identity fails.
This argument does not rely on analogy but on the nature of properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحققه أن الإرادة لو كانت هي الفعل، والفعل والمفعول عندهم واحد لكانت الإرادة هي الفعل وهو المراد، فكان السواد إرادة، وكذا البياض والحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق، والموت والجهل وجميع أنواع الأعراض، وكذا الجواهر والأجسام. ثم التقرير بعد هذا من وجهين أحدهما أن الإرادة مع أنها عند جميع أهل من خواص الحي كالعلم والقدرة وغيرهما لو كانت كذلك لجاز ذلك أيضا في العلم والقدرة وكان العلم هو المعلوم والقدرة هي المقدور، ولكان كل سواد وبياض وحركة وسكون وغير ذلك من الأعراض والجواهر والأجسام علما لله تعالى وقدرة وحيث لم يجز ذلك في العلم والقدرة لأنهما من خواص الحي، فلو كان كل معلوم علما وكل مقدور قدرة، وصار السواد والبياض وما وراءها من الأعراض والأجسام علما وقدرة مع أن تلك الأشياء ليست من خواص الحي، لخرج العلم والقدرة من أن يكونا من خواص الحي، وذلك ممتنع فامتنع أن يكون المعلوم علما، والمقدور قدرة، وكذا هذا في الإرادة. والتقرير الثاني أن الإرادة لما كانت من خواص الحي، والسواد والبياض وغيرهما لم تكن من خواصه، فلو كان المراد هو عين الإرادة، والمراد ليس من أخص أوصاف الحي، فإن السواد والبياض والاجتماع والافتراق وغيرهما من
And he confirms it: if will were the act, and act and object are one according to them, then will would be the act and it would be the object willed. So blackness would be will, and likewise whiteness, motion, rest, conjunction, separation, death, ignorance, and all types of accidents, and likewise substances and bodies. Then the settlement after this is from two aspects: one of them is that will, although according to all the people (of kalam) is among the properties of the living being like knowledge and power and others, if it were so, then that would also be permissible in knowledge and power, and knowledge would be the known, and power would be the object of power. And every blackness, whiteness, motion, rest, and other accidents, substances, and bodies would be knowledge of Allah and power. And since that is not permissible in knowledge and power because they are properties of the living being, so if every known were knowledge and every object of power were power, and blackness, whiteness, and what is beyond them of accidents and bodies became knowledge and power, while those things are not properties of the living being, then knowledge and power would cease to be properties of the living being. That is impossible, so it is impossible that the known is knowledge and the object of power is power. Likewise this applies to will. The second aspect: since will is among the properties of the living being, while blackness, whiteness, and others are not among its properties, so if the object willed were the very essence of will, and the object willed is not among the most specific attributes of the living being—for blackness, whiteness, conjunction, separation, and others are from...
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument. If one claims that God's will (irada) is identical to the act (i.e., the will itself is the created thing), then absurdities follow. First, if will were the act, then all accidents (a'rad) like blackness, whiteness, motion, etc., as well as substances (jawahir) and bodies (ajsam), would be will, which is nonsensical. Then Nasafi gives two formal arguments (taqrir). First argument: Will is a property (khasisa) of living beings, just like knowledge and power. If it is assumed that will is the same as the object willed (the act), then by analogy knowledge would be the same as the known, and power the same as the object of power. This would imply that all contingent things (blackness, whiteness, etc.) would be knowledge and power, but those things are not properties of living beings, so knowledge and power would cease to be distinctive properties of life—impossible. Therefore, will cannot be identical to the act. Second argument: Will is a property of the living being, but the objects willed (e.g., blackness, whiteness) are not properties of the living being. If the object willed were identical to will, then these objects would have to be properties of the living being, which they are not. Hence the identification fails. The text is cut off at the page break, but the argument is sufficiently conveyed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will might be identical with the act of creation, collapsing the distinction between the divine attribute and the temporal object.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if will were identical to the act, then every created thing would be will, which is absurd. Moreover, will is a property of the living being (like knowledge and power), but the objects willed are not, so they cannot be identical.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why can't the object willed (al-murad) be identical to will (al-irada)?
A) Because will is an accident, not a substance.
B) Because will is a property of the living being, but the objects willed are not.
C) Because the objects willed are multiple while will is one.
D) Because will is eternal while objects are temporal.

Key Takeaway
God's will is a distinct attribute inseparable from life, not identical to the temporal objects He creates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is not identical to the events in your life, how does that change the way you interpret both your blessings and your trials? Are you tempted to see every outcome as a direct expression of His will? What does this lesson say about the distinct role of your own choices?
?
The argument shows that identifying will with the act leads to absurdities like calling blackness 'will'. Have you ever mistakenly conflated God's will with something He created, perhaps making an idol out of a circumstance? How can this truth protect you from such confusion and deepen your reliance on God alone?
?
Nasafi states that will is a property of the living. Since you are alive by God's will, what does that imply about the sacredness of your life? Are you living in a way that honors the fact that your will—though created—reflects the attribute of the Living God? What one change can you make today to align your will more closely with His?

Divine Attributes

Argument from Analogy: Will, Knowledge, and Power
Nasafi presents a hypothetical and the first argument refuting the equation of irada with the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why identifying Allah's will with the act leads to absurd consequences, through an analogy with knowledge and power.

Core Idea
If will were the act, everything would be will — and that is impossible.

Nasafi begins by showing the unacceptable consequence of equating divine will with the act. He then draws a parallel with knowledge and power, which are also properties of the living. If the equation held, those attributes would also become identical to their objects, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
2
Identify the hypothetical scenario
Notice what would follow if irada were the act: everything would be irada. List the examples given.
3
Follow the analogy with knowledge and power
The same logic applied to knowledge and power would make them identical to their objects. Follow the reasoning step by step.
4
Recognise the impossibility
Why is it impossible for knowledge to be the known? Because knowledge is a property of the living, but objects are not. The same applies to will.
This is the core of the argument.
5
Conclude
Therefore, the initial equation is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحققه أن الإرادة لو كانت هي الفعل، والفعل والمفعول عندهم واحد لكانت الإرادة هي الفعل وهو المراد، فكان السواد (۱) إرادة، وكذا البياض والحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق، والموت والجهل وجميع أنواع الأعراض، وكذا الجواهر والأجسام. ثم التقرير بعد هذا من وجهين أحدهما أن الإرادة مع أنها عند جميع أهل من خواص الحي كالعلم والقدرة وغيرهما لو كانت كذلك (لجاز ذلك ) (٢) أيضا في العلم والقدرة وكان العلم هو المعلوم والقدرة هي المقدور، ولكان كل سواد وبياض وحركة وسكون وغير ذلك من الأعراض والجواهر والأجسام علمًا الله تعالى وقدرة وحيث لم يجز ذلك في العلم والقدرة لأنهما من خواص الحي، فلو كان كل معلوم علما وكل مقدور قدرة ، وصار السواد والبياض وما وراءها من الأعراض والأجسام علما وقدرة (۳) مع أن تلك الأشياء ليست من خواص الحي، لخرج (٤) العلم والقدرة () من أن يكونا من خواص الحي، وذلك ممتنع فامتنع أن يكون المعلوم علما، والمقدور قدرة، وكذا هذا في الإرادة. والتقرير الثاني أن الإرادة لما كانت من خواص الحي، والسواد والبياض وغيرهما لم تكن من خواصه، فلو كان المراد هو عين الإرادة، والمراد ليس من أخص أوصاف الحي، فإن السواد والبياض والاجتماع والافتراق وغيرهما من الأعراض ، ليست من خواص الحي . لخرجت الإرادة من أن تكون من أخص أوصاف الحي. فينبغي أن تتصف الجمادات والموات بالإرادة بطريق الحقيقة، وحيث لم يجر وكان ذلك محالا (دل) أن كون الإرادة) (۱) فعلا محال.
And it verifies that if irada (divine will) were the act, and the act and the object acted upon are one according to them, then irada would be the act and it would be the object willed. Thus blackness would be irada, likewise whiteness, motion, rest, conjunction, separation, death, ignorance, and all kinds of accidents, likewise atoms and bodies. Then the exposition after this is from two aspects: First, that irada, while according to all people it is among the properties of the living like knowledge and power and others, if it were so, then that would also be permissible in knowledge and power, so knowledge would be the known and power would be the powerful, and every blackness, whiteness, motion, rest, and other accidents, atoms, and bodies would be knowledge of Allah Almighty and power. And since that is not permissible in knowledge and power because they are properties of the living, if every known were knowledge and every powerful were power, and blackness, whiteness, and what follows them of accidents and bodies became knowledge and power, while those things are not properties of the living, then knowledge and power would exit from being properties of the living, and that is impossible. So it is impossible that the known be knowledge and the powerful be power. Likewise this in irada. The second exposition: Since irada is among the properties of the living, and blackness, whiteness, and others are not among His properties, if the object willed were the very irada, and the object willed is not among the most specific attributes of the living, for blackness, whiteness, conjunction, separation, and others of the accidents are not among the properties of the living, then irada would exit from being among the most specific attributes of the living. So it would be necessary that inanimate objects and dead things be described with irada in a real sense. And since this does not occur and is impossible, it indicates that irada being an act is impossible.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine will (irada) is not identical to the act. Nasafi begins by stating a hypothetical that if irada were the act, and act and object are one, then everything—accidents like blackness, whiteness, motion, rest, etc., and also atoms and bodies—would be irada. He then presents two arguments. The first argument draws an analogy with knowledge and power. If irada were the act, then by the same token knowledge would be the known and power the powerful, leading to absurdities that contradict the fact that knowledge and power are specific properties of the living. Since that is impossible, the same impossibility applies to irada being the act. The second argument focuses on the exclusive attribute of life. Irada is a property specific to living beings. But the objects willed (e.g., blackness, whiteness) are not properties of the living. If irada were the act (i.e., the object willed), then irada would no longer be a unique property of the living, and inanimate objects would also have irada, which is absurd. Therefore, irada cannot be the act.

Key Terms
إرادة
فعل
معلوم
مقدور
خواص الحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will is identical to the act He creates, so that the act itself is the will.
Correction: Nasafi shows this leads to absurdity: everything would be will, and by analogy knowledge and power would also become identical to their objects, which contradicts their nature as exclusive attributes of the living.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's first argument, why is it impossible for irada to be the act?
A) Because if irada were the act, then everything would be irada, which is absurd.
B) Because irada is a property of the living, and the act is not, so they cannot be identical.
C) Because the analogy with knowledge and power shows that if irada were the act, then knowledge would be the known, leading to an impossibility.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
The equation of irada with the act is impossible because it forces an absurd analogy with knowledge and power, which are also exclusive attributes of the living.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You pray for something, but it does not happen. Does the distinction between will and act in this lesson help you see that God's will is not bound to the act? How does that change your trust in Him even when your own desires are not fulfilled?
?
Think of a time you confused your own will with your action—you wanted to do something but did not. How does the absurdity of equating will with act in this passage reveal the importance of distinguishing between your intentions and your deeds?
?
This passage warns against conflating God's attributes with His objects. Are there attributes of Allah that you tend to think of as 'things' rather than as descriptions of His essence? How can you guard against this mistake in your own understanding?

Argument from the Exclusive Property of Life
Nasafi presents the second argument directly refuting the equation of irada with the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's second argument that divine will cannot be the act because will is an exclusive attribute of living beings, while the objects willed are not.

Core Idea
If will were the act, inanimate objects would have will — which is absurd.

Nasafi presents a second, more direct argument. Irada is a specific attribute of living beings, but the objects willed (like colors, motions) are not. If irada were identical to the act, then those objects would share in that attribute, contradicting its exclusivity.

Guided Path
1
Read the second exposition passage
This is a self-contained argument. Read the Arabic and translation.
2
Identify the premise
Irada is a property of the living (khawāṣṣ al-ḥayy). List the examples of what is not a property of the living.
3
Understand the conditional
If the object willed were the same as irada, then irada would no longer be exclusive because the objects are not exclusive properties of the living.
4
Draw the absurd consequence
If irada were the act, then inanimate objects would have irada in a real sense, which is impossible.
5
Conclude
Therefore, irada cannot be the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحققه أن الإرادة لو كانت هي الفعل، والفعل والمفعول عندهم واحد لكانت الإرادة هي الفعل وهو المراد، فكان السواد (۱) إرادة، وكذا البياض والحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق، والموت والجهل وجميع أنواع الأعراض، وكذا الجواهر والأجسام. ثم التقرير بعد هذا من وجهين أحدهما أن الإرادة مع أنها عند جميع أهل من خواص الحي كالعلم والقدرة وغيرهما لو كانت كذلك (لجاز ذلك ) (٢) أيضا في العلم والقدرة وكان العلم هو المعلوم والقدرة هي المقدور، ولكان كل سواد وبياض وحركة وسكون وغير ذلك من الأعراض والجواهر والأجسام علمًا الله تعالى وقدرة وحيث لم يجز ذلك في العلم والقدرة لأنهما من خواص الحي، فلو كان كل معلوم علما وكل مقدور قدرة ، وصار السواد والبياض وما وراءها من الأعراض والأجسام علما وقدرة (۳) مع أن تلك الأشياء ليست من خواص الحي، لخرج (٤) العلم والقدرة () من أن يكونا من خواص الحي، وذلك ممتنع فامتنع أن يكون المعلوم علما، والمقدور قدرة، وكذا هذا في الإرادة. والتقرير الثاني أن الإرادة لما كانت من خواص الحي، والسواد والبياض وغيرهما لم تكن من خواصه، فلو كان المراد هو عين الإرادة، والمراد ليس من أخص أوصاف الحي، فإن السواد والبياض والاجتماع والافتراق وغيرهما من الأعراض ، ليست من خواص الحي . لخرجت الإرادة من أن تكون من أخص أوصاف الحي. فينبغي أن تتصف الجمادات والموات بالإرادة بطريق الحقيقة، وحيث لم يجر وكان ذلك محالا (دل) أن كون الإرادة) (۱) فعلا محال.
And it verifies that if irada (divine will) were the act, and the act and the object acted upon are one according to them, then irada would be the act and it would be the object willed. Thus blackness would be irada, likewise whiteness, motion, rest, conjunction, separation, death, ignorance, and all kinds of accidents, likewise atoms and bodies. Then the exposition after this is from two aspects: First, that irada, while according to all people it is among the properties of the living like knowledge and power and others, if it were so, then that would also be permissible in knowledge and power, so knowledge would be the known and power would be the powerful, and every blackness, whiteness, motion, rest, and other accidents, atoms, and bodies would be knowledge of Allah Almighty and power. And since that is not permissible in knowledge and power because they are properties of the living, if every known were knowledge and every powerful were power, and blackness, whiteness, and what follows them of accidents and bodies became knowledge and power, while those things are not properties of the living, then knowledge and power would exit from being properties of the living, and that is impossible. So it is impossible that the known be knowledge and the powerful be power. Likewise this in irada. The second exposition: Since irada is among the properties of the living, and blackness, whiteness, and others are not among His properties, if the object willed were the very irada, and the object willed is not among the most specific attributes of the living, for blackness, whiteness, conjunction, separation, and others of the accidents are not among the properties of the living, then irada would exit from being among the most specific attributes of the living. So it would be necessary that inanimate objects and dead things be described with irada in a real sense. And since this does not occur and is impossible, it indicates that irada being an act is impossible.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine will (irada) is not identical to the act. Nasafi begins by stating a hypothetical that if irada were the act, and act and object are one, then everything—accidents like blackness, whiteness, motion, rest, etc., and also atoms and bodies—would be irada. He then presents two arguments. The first argument draws an analogy with knowledge and power. If irada were the act, then by the same token knowledge would be the known and power the powerful, leading to absurdities that contradict the fact that knowledge and power are specific properties of the living. Since that is impossible, the same impossibility applies to irada being the act. The second argument focuses on the exclusive attribute of life. Irada is a property specific to living beings. But the objects willed (e.g., blackness, whiteness) are not properties of the living. If irada were the act (i.e., the object willed), then irada would no longer be a unique property of the living, and inanimate objects would also have irada, which is absurd. Therefore, irada cannot be the act.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that God's will is the same as His act, so the act is nothing but will expressed.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if that were true, then will would not be an exclusive attribute of God, and even inanimate objects would have will, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise in Nasafi's second argument against identifying irada with the act?

B) Irada is a property exclusive to living beings, while the objects willed are not.
C) Irada is a single attribute, while acts are many.
D) The act is known by its effect, while irada is not.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's second argument proves that divine will cannot be the act because that would strip will of its exclusive status as a property of the living.

Refuting the Claim That Divine Will Means Command
Nasafi refutes equating irada with amr using verse and two absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine will (irada) cannot be equated with divine command (amr), and the two absurd consequences that follow from that equation.

Core Idea
Divine will and command are distinct: command can be disobeyed, will always occurs.

Some theologians claimed that when God's will relates to human actions, it simply means His command. Nasafi refutes this by showing that if irada were amr, it would lead to logical absurdities. He uses a Quranic verse and deduces two impossible consequences, confirming that will and command are separate attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Note the verse and the two arguments.
2
Understand the verse's implication
Nasafi quotes verse 10:99: 'If your Lord had willed, all would believe.' If irada meant command, the verse would become 'If your Lord commanded, all would believe.' Realize the shift.
The verse uses 'willed', not 'commanded'.
3
Examine the first absurd consequence
If irada were command, then those who didn't believe were not commanded, so they wouldn't be sinners for rejecting faith.
This contradicts the Islamic teaching that unbelievers are sinful.
4
Examine the second absurd consequence
If irada were command, then everyone God commands must become believers, but we see many who are commanded yet do not believe, which would be a contradiction in God's action.
God's command is obeyed by some, not all.
5
Conclude with Nasafi's verdict
Nasafi concludes that the claim is false and misguided. Understand why this distinction is crucial in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد بالإرادة ليس الأمر] ودليل بطلان ما قالوا أن إرادته تعالى إذا أضيفت إلى فعل العباد كان المراد منها الأمر قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ جَمِيعًا م (۲) فلو كانت الإرادة أمرا، لكان تقدير الآية ولو أمر ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلها جميعا. وفيه فساد من وجهين: أحدهما أنه يؤدي إلى أنه تعالى لم يأمر من لم يؤمن، ولو كان ذلك، لم يكن بترك الإيمان عاصيا . والآخر أن كل من أمره ينبغي أن يكون مؤمنا، ورأينا كثيرا ممن أمره ولم يؤمن، فكان فيه تناقض وتكذيب وذلك محال. فدل أن ما قاله هؤلاء باطل وضلال.
[What is meant by irada is not command.] And the proof of the invalidity of what they said: that His will, exalted, when added to the actions of servants, what is meant by it is command, is the saying of the Almighty: {And if your Lord had willed, all those on earth would have believed, all of them.} (10:99) [So] if irada were command, then the estimation of the verse would be: 'If your Lord commanded, all those on earth would have believed, all of them.' And in this is corruption from two aspects: First, it leads to the conclusion that the Almighty did not command those who did not believe, and if that were so, then one would not be a sinner for leaving faith. Second, everyone whom He commands ought to be a believer, but we see many whom He commands and who do not believe, thus involving contradiction and denial, and that is impossible. So it indicates that what these people said is false and misguided.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that when God's will (irada) is related to human actions, it simply means His command (amr). Nasafi first cites the verse from Sura Yunus: 'And if your Lord had willed, all on earth would have believed.' If irada were command, the verse would read: 'If your Lord commanded, all would believe.' He then shows two absurd consequences. First, it would imply that God did not command those who disbelieve, so they would not be sinners. Second, it would require that everyone God commands must become a believer, yet we see many who are commanded but do not believe. This would lead to contradiction and falsehood, which is impossible for God. Hence the claim is invalid and misguided.

عاصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will (irada) and His command (amr) are the same when it comes to human actions.
Correction: Nasafi shows they are distinct: will is always fulfilled, command can be disobeyed. Equating them leads to absurdities that contradict scripture and reason.

Checkpoint
What is the first absurd consequence Nasafi mentions if irada were command?
A) God would be commanding disbelief.
B) God would not have commanded those who disbelieve, so they would not be sinners.
C) Everyone would be forced to believe.
D) The verse would be contradictory.

Key Takeaway
Divine will (irada) is not the same as command (amr); will always occurs, but command can be disobeyed, and equating them leads to theological absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this reality shape your attitude toward those who reject faith—do you see them as merely objects of God's will, or as responsible sinners who disobey His command?
?
This lesson distinguishes between God's will (which always occurs) and His command (which can be disobeyed). Examine your own life: are there areas where you treat God's commands as optional, as if they were merely suggestions rather than binding orders that you are accountable to obey? What would change if you truly lived as someone who knows that every command carries the weight of divine authority and that disobedience is sin?
?
The second absurdity shows that many who are commanded do not believe. Do you find yourself among those who hear God's command but do not act? What specific command of God are you currently neglecting, and what will you do today to obey it, knowing that your disobedience is not a failure of God's will but a failure of your own response?

Refutation That Divine Will Is Desire
Nasafi refutes equation of will and desire, cites two Mu'tazili views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that divine will (irada) cannot be equated with desire (shahwa), and how two Mu'tazili theologians defined desire to further clarify the distinction.

Core Idea
God's will is not desire because desire implies need and benefit, which do not apply to Him.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a critical misunderstanding about the nature of divine will. Some might think that God's will is analogous to human desire, but Nasafi shows that desire is a specific type of will that seeks benefit or pleasure. Since God is completely free from need, His will cannot be desire. He then contrasts two Mu'tazili definitions of desire, further clarifying the concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand Nasafi's argument and the two reported views.
Notice that the passage ends abruptly with 'وهو' – the main refutation is still complete.
2
Identify Nasafi's core refutation
Find the sentence where Nasafi defines shahwa as a specific irada aimed at benefit and applies it to God. Understand why God's irada cannot be desire.
The key point: shahwa seeks benefit, but God is not benefited.
3
Examine al-Jubbai's distinction
Read al-Jubbai's claim that desire can coexist with aversion while will cannot. Study the examples of the thirsty faster and medicine taker.
This helps to show that desire and will are conceptually different.
4
Consider Abu Hashim's definition
Note Abu Hashim's definition of shahwa as involving sensory pleasure. Reflect on how this definition also excludes God, who has no senses.
Both definitions reinforce the idea that God's will is not desire.
5
Reflect on the theological importance
Ask yourself why it matters to distinguish divine will from desire. Consider how this preserves God's transcendence and perfection.
This distinction prevents anthropomorphism and upholds tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: إن الإرادة هي الشهوة قلنا : ليس على الإطلاق بل الشهوة إرادة مخصوصة وهي إرادة ما فيه نفع إما لذة وإما غيرها، والله تعالى لا ينتفع بشيء فلا تكون إرادته اشتهاء. وزعم الجبائي (۳) أن الشهوة للشيء قد تجامع الكراهية له. وإرادة الشيء لا تجامع كراهيته قال: ألا ترى أن الصائم الذي اشتد عطشه يشتهي شرب الماء ولا يريده ، وشارب الدواء يريد شربه ولا يشتهيه فكانت الشهوة عنده توقان النفس إلى الشيء. وزعم أبو هاشم (٤) أن الشهوة ما فيه لذة للحواس. وهو
And what they said: that irada is shahwa (desire). We say: not absolutely, rather shahwa is a specific irada, and it is volition of something that has benefit, either pleasure or other than it, and Allah Almighty is not benefited by anything, so His irada is not desiring. And al-Jubbai claimed that desire for a thing may coexist with aversion to it, while irada for a thing does not coexist with aversion to it. He said: Do you not see that the fasting person who is extremely thirsty desires to drink water but does not will it, and the one who drinks medicine wills to drink it but does not desire it? So according to him, shahwa is the yearning of the soul for something. And Abu Hashim claimed that shahwa is that which contains sensual pleasure. And it is...
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that divine irada is the same as shahwa (desire). Nasafi begins by stating that shahwa is a specific type of irada, namely the volition of something that brings benefit (pleasure or otherwise). Since God is not in need of any benefit, His irada cannot be desire. He then reports two Mu'tazili views. Al-Jubbai argued that desire for something can coexist with aversion to it, whereas irada does not; he gave the example of a thirsty faster who desires water but does not will it (because of the fast), and a medicine taker who wills the drink but does not desire it. Thus, for al-Jubbai, shahwa is the soul's yearning. Abu Hashim defined shahwa as that which involves sensory pleasure. The passage ends abruptly, but the main refutation is clear: divine irada cannot be reduced to desire because God is beyond benefit and pleasure.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God's will is similar to human desire, implying need or passion.
Correction: Nasafi shows desire necessarily involves seeking benefit or pleasure, which does not apply to God. Thus divine will is categorically different.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject that divine irada is shahwa (desire)?
A) Because desire always coexists with aversion.
B) Because desire is a specific type of irada that seeks benefit, and God is free from need.
C) Because desire is only for sensory pleasure.
D) Because desire is temporal and irada is eternal.

Key Takeaway
God's will is not desire; desire implies benefit and need, which are absent in God.

You serve a God who is completely free from need—He does not will things for His own benefit. Does your worship reflect that reality, or are you treating Allah as if He needs your prayers and obedience? Divine Will Is Not an Accident
Nasafi argues that irada cannot be an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine will (irada) cannot be classified as an accident (ʿaraḍ) based on the properties of living beings.

Core Idea
Accidents belong to the living; if irada were an accident, inanimate objects would have will.

This passage presents a concise logical argument against the view that divine will is an accident. Since accidents are specific to living beings, and inanimate objects are not described as having will, it follows that will cannot be an accident. Let's see how Imam Nasafi builds this proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the premise: accidents are properties of the living. Then trace the conclusion.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice the conditional reasoning: if irada were an accident, it would be among the specific attributes of the living. But inanimate objects do not have irada, so it cannot be an accident.
3
Consider the implication
This refutes those who claimed divine will is a created accident in Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأعراض ، ليست من خواص الحي . لخرجت الإرادة من أن تكون من أخص أوصاف الحي. فينبغي أن تتصف الجمادات والموات بالإرادة بطريق الحقيقة، وحيث لم يجر وكان ذلك محالا (دل) أن كون الإرادة فعلا محال.

المراد بالإرادة ليس الأمر]

ودليل بطلان ما قالوا أن إرادته تعالى إذا أضيفت إلى فعل العباد كان المراد منها الأمر قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُّهُمْ جَمِيعًا ﴾ فلو كانت الإرادة أمرا، لكان تقدير الآية ولو أمر ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلها جميعا. وفيه فساد من وجهين: أحدهما أنه يؤدي إلى أنه تعالى لم يأمر من لم يؤمن، ولو كان ذلك، لم يكن بترك الإيمان عاصيا . والآخر أن كل من أمره ينبغي أن يكون مؤمنا، ورأينا كثيرا ممن أمره ولم يؤمن، فكان فيه تناقض وتكذيب وذلك محال. فدل أن ما قاله هؤلاء باطل وضلال.
Accidents are not among the properties of the living. So irada (will) would be excluded from being among the most specific attributes of the living. Then it would be necessary that inanimate objects and dead things be described with irada in reality, and since this is not the case and that is impossible, it indicates that irada being an act is impossible.

What is meant by irada is not command.

And the proof of the falsity of what they said: that His irada, when added to the actions of slaves, means command is His saying exalted: 'And if your Lord had willed, all those on earth would have believed, all together.' So if irada were a command, then the interpretation of the verse would be: 'And if your Lord had commanded, all those on earth would have believed, all together.' And in this is corruption in two respects: one is that it leads to the consequence that He did not command the one who did not believe, and if that were the case, then he would not be disobedient by leaving belief. And the other is that everyone He commands ought to be a believer, but we see many whom He commands and they do not believe, so there is contradiction and denial, and that is impossible. So it indicates that what these people said is false and misguided.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage refutes two false views on divine will (irada). First, the author argues that irada cannot be an accident (ʿaraḍ) because accidents are properties of living things. If irada were an accident, it would be among the specific attributes of living beings, yet we do not describe inanimate objects with irada, so it cannot be an accident. The phrase 'كون الإرادة فعلا' likely means 'irada being an act/accident' is impossible.

Second, turning to the denial that irada is a command (amr), the author adduces a Quranic verse: 'If your Lord had willed, all on earth would have believed.' If irada meant command, the verse would read 'if your Lord had commanded...'—but then two absurdities follow: (1) God would not have commanded the unbelievers, so they would not be disobedient, and (2) everyone commanded would necessarily believe, which contradicts observable reality. Therefore the identification of irada with command is false.

Key Terms
إرادة
أعراض
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will is an accident (created attribute).
Correction: Accidents are properties of living bodies; Allah is not a body, and irada is an eternal attribute, not an accident.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise in the argument that irada cannot be an accident?
A) Accidents are properties of the living.
B) Allah's will is eternal.
C) Inanimate objects have no will.
D) The Quran proves irada is not an accident.

Key Takeaway
Divine will (irada) cannot be an accident because accidents are specific to living beings, and we do not attribute will to inanimate objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah’s will is always present and active, even when you feel He is silent? How does this knowledge change your patience in dua?
?
If inanimate objects cannot possess will, how much more should you, as a living being with will, use that will to submit to your Creator? What one area of your life needs immediate surrender?
?
The argument proves that Allah’s will is not a temporary event. Does your trust in His decree treat His will as eternal and unchanging, or do you sometimes wish His will would change to suit your desires?
